Abstract
District Cooling (DC) systems have showed to be a very attractive alternative to
common individual cooling systems.
Higher energy eciencies can generally be
obtained from a central cooling production, due to the possibility for having a mix
of dierent technologies available in the plant. One of these technologies is Aquifer
Thermal Energy Storage (ATES), from which groundwater can be used as a source
for cooling. This thesis investigates how DC and ATES can be integrated optimally
for a specic site in Copenhagen.
A thermodynamic model is developed to investigate the yearly performance of dier-
ent scenarios with respect to the operation of the ATES system. Similarly, a model
for a reference system is made in order to compare the integrated ATES and DC
with a conventional DC cooling plant with only ambient air as cold source. In ad-
dition, the dierent systems and operation scenarios are assessed from an economic
perspective.
The simulation model takes into account the hourly changes in cooling demand,
ambient air temperature, as well as groundwater temperature for a full year. The
ATES technology is utilised for cooling only, primarily in the summer. The heated
groundwater is immediately re-injected in another well.
In the winter time the
process is reversed and the aquifer is recharged with cold energy by using dry coolers,
for ensuring a thermal energy balance on a yearly basis.
The yearly coecient of performance (COP) for the reference system is found to be
5.08 for the overall cooling plant, with a yearly energy consumption of 1573.80 MWh
electricity. The thermodynamic simulations of dierent ATES operation scenarios
and system settings, showed that the best option is a peak load operation at cooling
demands above 1500 kW, in a system with 3 well-pairs. Here the overall yearly COP
is found to be 5.64, with a yearly energy consumption of 1417.49 MWh electricity.
This corresponds to energy savings of 9.9% compared to the reference system. It is
found that none of the investigated ATES scenarios are more economically feasible
than the reference system.

==PAGE:17==
Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
Copenhagen's goal of being the rst carbon neutral capital in 2025 implies several
initiatives for restructuring and optimising the energy sector [1]. The overall e-
ciency of the energy system in Denmark has for decades been improved by the use
of combined heat and power plants and district heating networks [2]. In recent years
the possibility of applying similar centralised production within the cooling sector
has been widely discussed, and recommendations for installing district cooling (DC)
systems are given on Danish as well as an European level [3,4].
Typical modern buildings in urban areas are designed with large glass facades, and
the number of oce workers are generally increasing  both of these facts are aspects
that contribute to higher indoor temperatures, and hence an increased demand for
cooling [5]. The Copenhagen 2025 Climate Plan entails the expansion of DC, which
is a smart way to signicantly limit the energy use related to the increased cooling
demand [1, 6].
The distribution systems for chilled water and the promotion of
DC can be based on knowledge and experiences from district heating [7], and the
approach of central cooling production allows feasible utilisation of natural cold
resources.
Many examples of successful district cooling projects can be found, with Stockholm
being one of the most prominent [8]. Also in the United States a large number of
DC systems was already used in the middle of the 1990s [9]. In Copenhagen, the
development of a DC network started in 2009, and today HOFOR District Cooling
has two cooling plants.
These DC plants in Copenhagen are highly dependent on seawater, which is used
for free cooling when its temperature is below 5.5◦C [10]. When its temperature is
below this threshold, the seawater is utilised as a heat sink for the condensation in
compression chillers. Typically, large cooling plants, e.g. for supermarkets, hospitals
etc., use a brine loop to carry condenser heat to a set of dry coolers, from where
the heat is released to the surrounding air [11]. This typical plant layout will hence
experience an extra temperature dierence (∆T) and extra energy use for the dry
coolers [12].
Even though a seawater DC systems might be more energy ecient, this system type
can sometimes be inferior to the typical dry cooler system when it comes to economy.
1
==PAGE:18==
1.2. Thesis statement
Large pipes and other equipment for pumping sea water from the harbour/coastline
to the cooling plant, constitute a large share of the initial investment [13]. The
feasibility of using sea water in a new cooling plant, will therefore probably strongly
depend on the distance to a seawater resource. Hence, it is necessary to investigate
alternative options for low temperature sources or heat sinks for DC plant locations
far away from the sea.
One potential alternative resource for cooling is groundwater. Thermal utilisation
of groundwater is primarily known from Aquifer Thermal Energy Storage (ATES)
systems, in which thermal energy can be stored seasonally in the aquifer [14]. Since
groundwater typically has a temperature below 10◦C , it might be suitable for im-
plementation in DC systems [15]. A potential requirement for thermal balance on a
yearly basis in the aquifer [16], causes some challenges for combining DC and ground-
water, since the winter operation of an ATES system (extraction of the stored heat)
might not be desirable for a DC company.
This project will analyse possible solutions for combining a DC system with the use
of groundwater as a cold source. The study will be based on a planned extension of
the current Copenhagen DC system, under which it is intended to supply district
cooling to businesses in Ørestad on Amager [17]. The development of a DC system
in Ørestad implies that a new cooling plant has to be built initially, due to the fact
that this area is quite far away from the current distribution network. A possible
interconnection might be implemented in the future, depending on locations of new
customers being connected over the coming years.
1.2
Thesis statement
The overall thesis statement is formulated as follows:
How is groundwater optimally implemented and utilised in a district cooling
system, considering energy eciency and private economy, in a yearly perspective?
The overall thesis statement will be answered on the basis of a set of sub-questions:
1. To which extend can ATES systems be implemented in the case area, and which
groundwater temperature and ow rate can be expected?
2. What is the expected demand variations over a typical year for the case area?
3. Which system conguration provides the most optimal system performance
from an energy perspective?
4. Which system conguration provides the lowest investment and operational
costs?
5. How can the plant operation be adjusted to optimally utilise the groundwater?
1.3
Approach and thesis structure
Inter-seasonal storage capabilities is one of the main advantages for ATES systems.
In addition, it might be a legal or operative requirement to maintain a yearly heat
balance in the aquifer. At the same time the cooling demand in DC systems typically
have signicant variations over the year.
2
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Due to these yearly variations related to the DC and ATES technologies, the thesis
work is approached with a yearly perspective in mind. The energy performance of
the included system designs will be investigated through thermodynamic simulations
for a full year, with an hourly resolution.
A cash ow based economic assessment is conducted on the basis of typical costs
for the considered types of technologies to determine if investment in integration
of ATES and district cooling is economically feasible. The economic analysis will
mainly consider the investment project from a private-economic perspective.
Theory and practice for DC and ATES systems is investigated, in order to obtain
an appropriate basis for the simulations and calculations. This is conducted via
literature studies and interviews with operators of existing systems.
The thesis report follows the Introduction, Methods, Results, and Discussion (IM-
RAD) structure.
For some sections additional information and material can be
found in the appendices.
1.4
Background
This section provides a preliminary description of the systems and technologies under
consideration, the case area, as well as regulations and specic issues that should be
taken into account with respect to thermal utilisation of the groundwater.
1.4.1
District cooling and thermal groundwater systems
District cooling
District cooling (DC) can be dened as a system in which chilled water is produced
at a central location, and then distributed in pipes to connected buildings that have a
cooling demand. A DC system can be described from its three major subsystems: A
central plant producing chilled water, a distribution network in which chilled water is
circulated, and substations at the connected buildings [18]. The central production
allows utilisation of a variety of cooling technologies, which can increase the overall
eciency compared to individual cooling systems [19]. Figure 1.1 provides a general
overview of a DC system.
The chilled water is normally supplied to customers at a xed temperature. This
is typically in the region of 6◦C -10◦C [20].
Similarly, a DC system is designed
for a specied return temperature from the customers which is typically 10◦C -
16◦C . From the system operator perspective, it is desired that the realised return
temperature is as close as possible to the design value. This will lower the necessary
ow rates in the distribution system, and thereby improve of the eciency of the DC
system [7,18]. For HOFOR District Cooling the system is operated with a supply
temperature of 6◦C and a desired return temperature of 16◦C [21].
The chilled water production at the central cooling plant is typically handled by
using one or more of the three main cooling technologies: Free cooling from a cold
source, mechanical vapour compression cooling, and absorption cooling [18]. The
absorption technology uses high temperature heat as the main driver for the cooling
3
==PAGE:20==
1.4. Background
Figure 1.1: General overview of major components in a DC system. Source: ECOHEATCOOL [5].
production.
Excess steam from power plants can be used for this purpose, but
a viable operation of absorption chillers requires low heat prices. Due to various
reasons it is in Denmark estimated that the absorption technology will play a minor
role in future DC systems [3].
The vapour compression technology is the most widely used within refrigeration
[23,24]. A vapour compression chiller is utilising the principle of moving heat from
a colder to a hotter source, by applying mechanical work to the cycle. The work is
supplied via the electricity driven compressor. Vapour compression chillers for larger
systems can generally obtain higher eciencies than smaller individual systems. It
is found in literature that a typical average coecient of performance (COP) for
a centralised electrically driven DC plant is 4.1-5.0, whereas individual building
cooling systems have an average COP of 2.1-2.9 [25]. Another publication states
a typical COP range of 4.7-6.75 for electric chiller based DC plants [18]. A main
reason why the centralised cooling production can obtain higher eciencies is that
it is easier to install multiple chiller units with dierent sizes to meet various load
conditions most optimally. This approach can be further enhanced with one or more
units having variable frequency drive to improve the eciency at part load.
Free cooling is a method under which the desired cooling eect can be obtained
by directly exchanging heat between the medium to be cooled and a cold source.
The cold source must have a temperature lower than the desired temperature, e.g.
the supply temperature of chilled water in a DC system. The cold source can be
air, seawater, lake-water, groundwater etc. [3,13,26]. One of the advantages of the
aqueous mediums is a higher specic heat capacity compared to air, which means
that less auxiliary energy is required (for pumps/fans).
Thermal groundwater systems
In addition to conventional drinking water supply, the groundwater can constitute
a resource for utilisation in thermal energy systems. The idea of thermal ground-
water systems started in the 1950s in China, and from the 1970s the technology has
been subject to research and implementation in the Western part of the world [27].
4
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Widespread use of thermal groundwater systems have been limited in Denmark,
but in the last decade the interest has increased. The general transition towards
implementation of technologies that can increase energy saving has promoted ther-
mal groundwater systems. Especially the possibility for using the groundwater as
a seasonal thermal energy storage, and as a coupling between district cooling and
heating, have put thermal groundwater systems in the spotlight [3].
This particular functionality of seasonal storage is utilised in an Aquifer Thermal
Energy Storage (ATES) system. Two groundwater wells are coupled with pipes to
a heat exchanger at ground level. The groundwater is pumped from one well to the
other, and on the way heat is either extracted from or rejected to the groundwater
via the heat exchanger. For an ATES system the wells are dedicated as "hot" and
"cold" [28]. This means that in a summer situation where it is desired to obtain a
cooling eect, e.g. air conditioning for a building, the groundwater is extracted from
the cold well and used as a cooling medium. This situation is illustrated in gure
1.2. The cooling load ˙Qcool is supplied to the groundwater in the heat exchanger,
and the groundwater is then injected to the hot well.
Hot well
Cold well
Heat exchanger
Hot well
Cold well
Heat exchanger
Figure 1.2: Schematic of an ATES system in summer/cooling operation mode.
The heat extracted to obtain the desired cooling eect is then stored in the aquifer.
Depending on the characteristics of the aquifer, the heat (or most of it) can be stored
here for a given period of time, which most often provides the option for seasonla
storage.
In wintertime when the ambient air temperature is low, the aquifer can be recharged,
i.e. cooled down to the original groundwater temperature (or to some degrees below).
This is done by reversing the groundwater ow in the system, which means that
groundwater is now pumped from the hot well, cooled down by the ambient (cold)
air with a recharge heat rate of ˙Qrech, and then injected in the cold well. This
process is illustrated in gure 1.3.
When the system is operated in winter/recharge mode for suciently many hours,
the cold well will be ready to supply cooling again in the following summer period.
The processes described above is for a system that solely utilises the groundwater
for cooling. The viability of this operation scheme is potentially justied from the
fact that cold energy from the ambient winter air is 'stored' in the aquifer and used
5
==PAGE:22==
1.4. Background
Hot well
Cold well
Heat exchanger
Hot well
Cold well
Heat exchanger
Figure 1.3: Schematic of an ATES system in winter/recharge operation mode.
in summertime when cooling demand is high. Hence, it can substitute more energy
consuming cooling technologies that would otherwise be used in the summer.
On the other hand, if the heat rejected to the ambient in wintertime, ˙Qrech, is used
as a low temperature source in heat pumps for district heating, the overall utilisation
and energy eciency of the system would be improved.
The ATES technology has been implemented in enormous extend in the Nether-
lands, with more than 1500 individual systems installed [3]. Only few examples of
district energy systems using ATES in the Netherlands are identied, which means
that a huge number of systems are individually owned and operated [29]. If well
locations and operation schemes are not coordinated appropriately between neigh-
bouring ATES systems, issues that lowers the system eciencies might occur. In
relation to avoidance of potential planning and operational issues (see section 1.4.5),
a potential increase of ATES system implementation in Denmark is expected to ben-
et from a combination with district energy systems.
Finally, it should be underlined that ATES is considered a very mature technology
based on extensive research and implementation in dierent parts of the world [29].
At the same time very high performance ratings are claimed in publications with
COP values from 22.9 to as high as 58 [3032].
1.4.2
Case area description
The specic case area selected for this thesis is Ørestad located on Amager, south-
east of Copenhagen city centre. The area is a newly developed city area, which
consists of many commercial buildings as well as residential apartments. The area
is very attractive to businesses [33], due to a location that oers easy access to the
city centre, the airport and at the same time provides scenic views to the protected
natural areas of Vestamager. Especially the great transport facilities enhance the
attractiveness for national and international companies [34].
Based on the high density of businesses the area might have a good potential for
district cooling customers. However, this very general statement needs to be inves-
tigated in more detail before a potential DC roll out is conducted [35].
6
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 1. Introduction
The fact that the area is newly developed might improve the general stance towards
energy ecient solutions, and hence increase the number of businesses that might
be interested in connecting to a potential future DC system. This applies both in
the attitudinal and practical sense. Companies might desire to improve their public
image by utilising sustainable energy supply solutions. At the same time a potential
transition to a new cooling solution might be a more obvious to a company when
they are building a new domicile or moving to new facilities, compared to if they were
staying in their existing buildings in older districts of the city. Furthermore, many
commercial buildings that are constructed in new city areas have large glass facades
and good insulation due to new standards for indoor light and comfort [20,23]. This
will also increase the demand for cooling in the summertime due to a high heating
impact from sunlight.
Based on the present composition of companies and buildings located in the area,
three dierent building types are assumed connected to the DC network considered
for this thesis: Hotel, Oce, and Mall.
With regards to the use of groundwater for the DC system, the case area is located
outside drinking water interests, which are areas that are identied and dened by
authorities. As will be described in greater details in section 1.4.3, this is a key
element in relation to obtain the necessary permissions for implementation of wells
for thermal utilisation of groundwater.
Three individual ATES systems are already installed in Ørestad, comprising Ram-
bøll, Crowne Plaza and DR-byen [36,37].
1.4.3
Drinking water interests
The groundwater in Denmark is a valuable resource and the country's drinking wa-
ter supply is exclusively based thereon [38]. This is a rare situation in comparison
with other European countries, where it is necessary to also use surface water that
require more complex water treatment before it can be supplied to citizens. Over-
exploitation of the aquifers is sometimes a problem in the capital area [38]. Even
though ATES systems re-inltrate the pumped groundwater to the same aquifer, im-
plementation of these systems will constitute a risk to the groundwater quality, both
with respect to potential contamination and the thermal imbalances that might oc-
cur in the aquifer. Hence, it is important to take these considerations about drinking
water into account when planning locations for thermal utilisation of groundwater,
in order to limit the overall impacts.
The general opinion of HOFOR District Cooling is that utilisation of groundwater for
cooling and heating purposes should be implemented only in areas without drinking
water interests. This view is in line with the governmental desire to retain a drinking
water supply which is solely based on pure groundwater that only needs simple
ltration, aeration and pH-adjustment [38].
The intended location for the district cooling system utilising groundwater is Ørestad
City and Ørestad Syd on the western side of Amager. A map showing the areas of
drinking water interests have been retrieved from the Danish Natural Environment
Portal to identify the risk of interfering with aquifers intended for drinking water
7
==PAGE:24==
1.4. Background
purposes [40]. The map can be seen in gure 1.4, where the case area is indicated by
a red line. The light blue shading indicates area with drinking water interests, and
the darker blue shading layer illustrates areas with special drinking water interests.
Figure 1.4: Areas with drinking water interests near the Ørestad and existing water supply systems.
Source: Danish Natural Environment Portal [40,41].
As seen in gure 1.4 the case area is located outside both categories of areas with
drinking water interests. The eastern border of the case area is adjacent to an area
with drinking water interests, in which a number of existing water supply wells are
located. However, based on a long distance to most of them it is assumed that
interference will be minimal.
In addition, this neighbouring area with drinking
water interests is only a class 2 area, i.e. no special drinking water interests, which
is expected to enhance the probability of obtaining permission to make the intended
groundwater wells for ATES in Ørestad.
1.4.4
Legislative conditions
Groundwater installations
The Danish legislation on groundwater wells and boreholes is based on the Dan-
ish Environmental Protection Act [42], and specic rules on how a well should be
constructed are given in Order on the Execution and Abandonment of Boreholes
8
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 1. Introduction
and Wells on Land [43]. This order is elaborated in the Guidance for Boreholes on
Land [44].
Specic requirements and recommendations are given in the above-mentioned doc-
uments with regards to arrangements in well top-housing, accessibility for mainte-
nance, possibilities for establishment of protection zone around the well, nalisation
of terrain etc. A more detailed description on this is provided in appendix A.1. In
addition, it should be highlighted that ATES systems are obliged to have automatic
measurement equipment for temperatures, pressures and extraction ow rates [45].
Operation of thermal groundwater systems
The legal requirements with respect to the operational conditions for an ATES
system are given in Order on Heat Recovery Systems and Groundwater Cooling
Systems [45]. Selected requirements are mentioned here.
The minimum temperature of the injected water should be 2◦C on a monthly average
basis. The peak temperatures must not exceed 25◦C and the maximum temperature,
measured as an average over a month, should be below 20◦C . The groundwater re-
injections must not inuence existing water supply systems and groundwater cooling
systems in such a way that the temperature of extracted water in existing systems
is increased by more than 0.5◦C .
Once a year, data on temperatures and volumes of extracted and injected ground-
water should be handed over to the authorities.
1.4.5
Main risks and issues for ATES systems
The execution of a successful ATES system depends on a lot of parameters in re-
lation to the properties of the aquifer. The general idea of gaining access to an
aqueous resource of almost constant natural temperature over the year is attrac-
tive for developers of thermal energy systems like DC, when the temperature of the
source is at a suitable level for the specic case. However, the risks involved with
the planning and operation of an ATES system might be too large for an energy
company to implement this groundwater technology. This section will in short go
through the main risks that needs to be taken into account in the planning phase
and during the operation of the system.
Risks during the planning period
The rst essential requirement for the execution of an ATES system is to obtain
the relevant permissions from the local authorities. Furthermore it is necessary to
conduct a pre-investigation of a given site to determine if the aquifer is suitable for
ATES. It is desired to obtain a characterisation of the aquifer with respect to geology,
aquifer thickness, hydraulic properties, ow, thermal properties, temperatures and
chemistry [14, 46]. This pre-investigation is typically handled by a company with
specialisation in subsurface investigations and typically they oer an agreement
which is dived into appropriate phases [47]. This serves to limit the economic loss,
if it early in the process turns out that the site is not appropriate for ATES. Further
details are provided in appendix A.2.
9
==PAGE:26==
1.4. Background
In some cases sucient information about the aquifer might be available from earlier
groundwater projects, but most often it is necessary to perform exploratory drilling
with borehole-logging, sampling for chemical analysis etc. Subsequently, modelling-
based analyses of the groundwater are typically performed with inputs from the eld
measurements [14,47]. This necessary exhaustive subsurface pre-investigation is an
initial economic burden, which might end up with the conclusion that the ATES
project should be abandoned.
Risks related to operation
One issue that inuences the operation of an ATES system is the groundwater ow
rate and direction in the aquifer. If the horizontal ow is too high, the abilities
of energy storage in the aquifer might be very limited and the ATES system will
not function as intended [14]. On the other hand, if the system is intended to only
operate in cooling mode, a high ow in the aquifer is desirable. In this case it is
important that the hot well is located downstream from the cold well [28].
A related issue and maybe one of the most crucial risks related to the operation of an
ATES system is the phenomenon of thermal breakthrough. A thermal breakthrough
can be described as undesired interference between groundwater around the hot and
the cold well. The summer/cooling operation mode is used as an example here: Cold
water is extracted from the cold well, then it is heated in the heat exchanger, and
afterwards injected in the hot well. Over time it might occur that the injected hot
water will propagate towards the cold well and hereby cause a thermal breakthrough.
Figure 1.5 shows an example of this phenomenon.
Figure 1.5: Example of thermal breakthrough between the hot and the cold well in an ATES system.
Source: Banks (2009) [28].
The consequences of a thermal breakthrough is lower cooling eciency because of
requirement for higher pumping ow rates, and a worst case situation might be
system failure [14, 28]. The risk of thermal breakthrough is larger for system with
seasonal heat imbalance in the well-pair, e.g. a system that only pump from the cold
to the hot well. Also the pumping rate and the distance between the wells have an
inuence on the occurrence of thermal breakthrough. A publication suggests that
the system is operated with seasonal heat balance, the pumping rate is limited when
possible, and that the well separation is increased [28].
10
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2
Methodology
2.1
Ground water investigation
District cooling in Copenhagen from HOFOR have until now been based on sea
water as the only natural cooling medium. For the establishment of a successful dis-
trict cooling system in the case area Ørestad on Amager it is essential to investigate
alternative options for a cold natural resource, due to a long distance to the Copen-
hagen harbor and coastline. The overall focus of this thesis is to investigate whether
groundwater constitutes a feasible option for this purpose. This will largely depend
on the properties of the groundwater reservoir. Hence, an initial investigation will
determine the expected potential of the groundwater in the case area. The cooling
potential is of primary interest for this thesis, since the main function of the ATES
technology will be utilisation of 'cold energy' for the DC system.
2.1.1
Mapping of possible locations for groundwater wells
An initial simple screening of the case area is intended to provide an overview of
potential specic sites for groundwater wells. The planned location of the district
cooling plant is the address Fælleddiget 2, 2200 Copenhagen S [49], which is in the
north-eastern corner of the case area. The main distribution pipes are planned to
be placed along a water channel, that separates the case area from the detached
residential houses to the east. For more information on the case are, please see
section 1.4.2.
The screening will identify available land areas, assess the owner rights and possible
future construction plans. This serves to identify the most probable locations for
the wells for the ATES system. At the same time the screening will provide an
initial expectation of the number of well-pairs that can be considered realistic to be
implemented.
2.1.2
Critical risks related to the establishment of ATES
There are signicant uncertainties associated with planning of an ATES system. The
main issue is the lack of knowledge about the geological and hydrological conditions
of a specic site or area. This means that the cooling/heating potential from the
11
==PAGE:28==
2.1. Ground water investigation
groundwater aquifer is unknown, and the business case of the project will be very
sensitive to the related parameters.
Risk assessment and pre-investigation with regards to the groundwater reservoirs
for the desired ATES project in Ørestad is out of scope of this thesis.
Hence,
the cooling potential of ATES in the case area is estimated with the best possible
degree of certainty by the use of database analysis and other empirical information
from owners and operators of existing water supply and ATES systems on Amager.
The two main parameters that inuences the cooling potential is the groundwater
temperature and ow rate that can be obtained from each well. The methodology
for obtaining these parameters will be given in section 2.1.3 and 2.1.4.
2.1.3
Expected natural groundwater temperatures
The natural temperature of the groundwater is generally considered to be constant
at a level of 9-10◦C for an ATES system, which implies depths up to 100 m [15,50]. If
the groundwater surface is more than 5 meters below terrain level, a rule of thumb is
that the groundwater temperature is constant around 8◦C [51]. Even numbers as low
as 6-7◦C for the natural groundwater temperatures have been reported by owners of
existing ATES systems. The ocial number given by the drinking water department
of HOFOR is 8◦C .
The temperature will of course vary depending on several
parameters, such as groundwater ow rate and direction, geological composition,
distance to the ocean, other nearby groundwater wells for drinking water and ATES
etc.
Even though the dierences between the numbers presented above seems to be
small, it is important to take into account that such temperature dierences will
signicantly inuence the degree of utilisation for cooling purposes. Figure 2.1 shows
the principle of simple heat exchange between groundwater and DC distribution
water.

Figure 2.1: Illustration of heat exchange between groundwater and DC distribution water.
The possible supply temperature of the chilled DC distribution water is naturally
limited by the temperature of extracted groundwater, i.e. TDC,sup > Tgw,1. With the
temperature set on the considered DC system of TDC,sup = 6◦C and TDC,ret = 16◦C, it
is evident that groundwater (with the temperature ranges presented in the beginning
of this section) can not be used alone for the cooling production. From the scale of
the temperatures, it also follows that a few degrees of uncertainty in groundwater

temperature will have a large impact on the amount of cooling that can be supplied
by natural means from the aquifer.
Hence, the groundwater temperature will largely inuence the amount of conven-
tional cold water production that the ATES technology can substitute. Many ex-
isting ATES systems are found to operate with a higher temperature set on the
distribution side, e.g. 12/18◦C , which thermodynamically enables cold supply wa-
ter to be solely produced from exchanging heat with the groundwater.

The ATES system will store thermal energy in the aquifer. This will imply that the
groundwater temperature at the wells will be dierent from the natural occurring
temperature in some periods of the year. The approach for taking this into account
in the modelling is described in section 2.8.

Another important parameter to assess for an ATES system is the achievable ex-

ground water for cooling as illustrated in gure 2.4.

Groundwater is pumped from the cold well with temperature T1, heated in the heat

equation 2.1.
˙Qcool = ˙mgw · cp,gw · (T2 −T1)
(2.1)
Here ˙Qcool is the heat transfer rate supplied to the groundwater, and cp,gw is the

The relationship between the mass ow rate of extracted groundwater and the heat
transfer rate is only linear. But due to signicant variations for the possible ex-
traction groundwater ow rate from location to location, this is still an important
parameter that needs to be assessed for the specic case.
The extracted groundwater ow rate should not be confused with the natural occur-
ring groundwater ow: The former is the ow rate of ground water pumped through
the ATES system/pipes, whereas the latter is the groundwater run-o or horizontal
ow occurring naturally within the aquifer.
The natural occurring groundwater ow within the aquifer will inuence the obtain-
able extraction ow rate. A detailed analysis of natural groundwater ow within
the aquifer is out of the scope of this thesis.
In stead, it is assumed that data
from other ATES systems on Amager is appropriate to used for this case. Detailed
cosiderations are provided in appendix A.4.

2.2
Cooling demand
The total cooling demand in a district cooling (DC) system is typically uctuating
over the year. Hence, in order to perform yearly simulations, it is necessary to take
these variations into account. The method used for obtaining a representative yearly
prole for the cooling demand is described in this section.
15
==PAGE:32==
2.2. Cooling demand
The starting point with respect to the total cooling demand is the customers con-
nected to the network. The customers in a district cooling (DC) system typically
have dierent distribution of their cooling demand. For this thesis three dierent
customer types are assumed to be connected to the system, in accordance with the
building types described in section 1.4.2, i.e. hotel, oce and mall. These all have
dierent consumption patterns which will be taken into account in the creation of
a yearly prole for the total cooling demand.
The cooling energy consumption can be divided into two parts. That is, one part
which is constant throughout the year, and one part which is uctuating due to
weather variations [8]. The former part of the cooling demand is here referred to
as base load, whereas the latter is called comfort load. The comfort load consists
of cooling for air-conditioning and similar purposes, and it is commonly assumed
that the comfort load can be directly related to the ambient air temperature [5].
Dierent suggestions are given for the European average temperature at which the
comfort cooling starts. The ECOHEATCOOL project from 2006 assumes that the
comfort cooling load starts at an ambient air temperature of 17◦C [5].
A more
recent investigation under the RESCUE project from 2014 nds that the average
start temperature for comfort cooling in Europe is around 10◦C based on measured
data from district cooling networks in European cities [54].
In this thesis, the cooling demand distribution over the year will be created from
existing proles used in the publicly available tool called "Fjernkøl 2.0" [55]. Hence,
the ambient air temperature at which the comfort cooling starts, will be dened
from these proles. The proles are based on building energy simulations from the
software "Bsim", and the tool includes 9 dierent building types.
The building
types are constructed on the basis of two dierent parameters: A g-value or solar
transmittance (solar heat gain through windows), and a value for the internal load
(heat gain from humans, electrical equipment, lighting etc.), where each of those
variables are assigned with 3 dierent values (low, medium and high). The cooling
demand prole for each of the included buildings for the considered case area is
chosen according to table 2.2. Notice that only the proles used for yearly distribu-
tion will only involve the comfort load. The base load is assumed to be a uniformly
distribution, which will be added to the uctuating comfort load to achieve the nal
cooling demand prole.
Table 2.2: Selection of demand proles from the tool Fjernkøl 2.0 [55].

40 (high)
Prole 8
The values (low, medium or high) for solar transmittance (g-value) and internal load
are assigned to the three building types based on best guesses. The low g-value is
chosen for the oce building, since it is expected that most working environments
nowadays will have some sort of shading to limit sunlight disturbances in the oces.
The medium value is chosen for the hotel and mall, since it is expected that most new
16
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 2. Methodology
buildings in Denmark will have modern insulated glass in the windows with more
than one glass layer, which will reduce the g-value. With regards to the internal
load, the oce is expected to be well described by the high value due to a high
number of electrical equipment such as printers, PCs, servers, and people. The mall
is similarly given the high value, due to heat load from a high number of persons,
whereas the hotel have been given the medium value. These choices result in the
proles as given in the rightmost column of table 2.2.
The described demand proles above are initially obtained in a dimensionless form
with unity appearing at the hour of maximum cooling load (peak of the dimensionless
demand prole).
The nal demand proles will then appear when the required
cooling capacity for the building is multiplied on the dimensionless prole for every
hour in the year.
The required cooling capacity for each building type will be determined on the basis
of a typical specic capacity, i.e. in the units W/m2, for buildings connected to a
DC system. An investigation of DC customer data at dierent locations in Europe
contains data from 11 oce buildings in the DC system in Gothenburg, Sweden [54].
This location is assumed to be representative for Copenhagen. The analysis present
both a specic capacity related to the total demand and the base load demand. The
specic cooling capacity refers to square-meters of cooled space. Hence, the total
cooled space will be established on the basis of assumptions on oor area of each
building type and a number of buildings for each type. Since the specic cooling
capacities relates to oce buildings, the values can only be directly applied to one
of the considered building types. It has not been possible to obtain typical cooling
capacities for hotels and malls, and hence the values assigned for these building types
are manually adjusted with the oce-values as a starting point. The assumptions
made for determination of the overall cooling capacity of the DC system are given
in table 2.3.

2.3
Ambient air temperature
The previous section described the cooling demand prole for a full year, where
the comfort cooling demand was introduced as being dependent on the ambient
air temperature. The cooling system to be investigated utilise dry coolers, where
the heat extracted in the cooling plant will be sent into the ambient air. Hence,
the air temperature will largely inuence the thermal performance of the cooling
plant. An air temperature prole to be used as input to the simulation model will
be determined in this section.
A commonly used reference for yearly energy simulations for dierent purposes is the
Design Reference Year (DRY). A Danish edition with various weather data was last
published in 2013 with subdivision of Denmark into ve to six regions and continuous
data collection in the period 2001-2010 [56]. An older version of the Danish DRY
was published in 1995, where the data collection is based on selected measurements
in the period 1975-1989 on a limited number of measuring stations [57]. The most
recent DRY would be preferable to use for this thesis, since the climate has probably
changed over the years and because the degree of uncertainty might have decreased
with the sectionalised of the country in the newer version. The datasets for ambient
air temperature for the two versions of DRY are shown together in gure 2.8.
Figure 2.8: Comparison of ambient air temperature from the Design Reference Year (DRY) 1995
and DRY 2013.
The temperature proles is seen to roughly follow each other with a limited number
of large deviations. The dimensionless comfort cooling distribution from Fjernkøl
2.0 used for the total cooling demand proles, constructed and shown in section
2.2, are made using DRY 1995. To be consistent and to avoid potential undesirable
20
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 2. Methodology
osets in the simulations, the temperature prole from DRY 1995 is used as input
to the simulation model. This means that the peak load identied in gure 2.6 will
occur the same hour of the year as for the highest air temperature.
The minimum air temperature for DRY 1995 is -21.1◦C , the average is 8.9◦C , and
the maximum is 32.1◦C .
2.4
The vapour compression cycle
In a simple form, a vapour compression system has four main components between
which a refrigerant is circulated: An evaporator, a compressor, a condenser and an
expansion device. Figure 2.9 shows a diagram of a vapour compression system used
as a water chiller.
Cooling medium inlet
Cooling medium outlet
Water outlet (chilled)
Water inlet
1
2
3
4
Low pressure
High pressure
Cooling medium inlet
Cooling medium outlet
Water outlet (chilled)
Water inlet
1
2
3
4
Low pressure
High pressure
Figure 2.9: Schematic of a vapour compression system for chilled water production.
The refrigerant is continuously circulated through state 1-4 shown in gure 2.9. The
thermodynamic processes occurring can be described as follows.
 The refrigerant is evaporated at low pressure from state 4 to 1 by extracting
heat ( ˙Qevap) from the water to be cooled.
 The refrigerant is mechanically compressed to a higher pressure from state 1
to 2, based on electricity input ( ˙Wcomp).
 The refrigerant is condensed at high pressure from state 2 to 3 by transferring
heat ( ˙Qcond) to a cooling medium.
 The refrigerant is expanded from state 3 to 4, changing from high to low
pressure.
The basic vapour compression cycle is often expanded to more complex system
layouts, depending on the application. In such cases the description of the thermo-
dynamic processes that the refrigerant undergoes will be slightly dierent.
21
==PAGE:38==
2.5. System design
2.5
System design
Two main systems are considered for this thesis. A conventional district cooling
plant, determined on the basis of most common technologies, will constitute a ref-
erence system. The second system includes the same principles as the reference
system, but with added features that involves utilisation of groundwater. The two
systems will be described in this section.
Note that the purpose of the diagrams presented here is to illustrate some main
principles of the cooling plant design. A detailed PI-diagram for a fully designed
plant might have more than one of the main components depending on the capacities,
special requirements etc. Only some of the main components are included in the
PI-diagrams here, and a full overview of a state of the art cooling plant would be
more complex.
2.5.1
Conventional cooling plant (reference system)
The reference system is a cooling plant with chillers and free coolers as main compo-
nents. These two are arranged in the system such that they can cool the distribution
water in the DC system on one side, and with a brine loop on the other side which
serves to carry away the transferred heat to the surroundings via dry coolers.
A proposed system design is illustrated from af PI-diagram in gure 2.10.
Brine loop and dry coolers
The upper part of the PI-diagram in gure 2.10 illustrates that the free cooler and
the condenser are connected to a common brine (or secondary refrigerant) system,
as shown with dashes lines. This enables exible utilisation of the installed dry
coolers, since both free and mechanical cooling can use the same dry cooler racks.
In cases where the chiller and free cooler are used simultaneously, the streams of hot
brine returned from the condenser and the free cooler are mixed before entering the
dry coolers.
The fans in the dry coolers are normally temperature controlled with a temperature
transmitter (TT) located at the brine outlet, and main pump in the brine loop is
controlled by the actual cooling capacity demand from chillers and free coolers [58].
A modulating electric valve can be placed at both of the forward streams to the
condenser and free cooler. The valve could be controlled by a TT located just in
front of the inlet to the given heat exchanger, as depicted in gure 2.10. As the
temperature gets lower, the valve will close and reduce the ow.
For practical reasons, a brine bypass is installed at both the condenser and the
free cooler. For the condenser this bypass serves to ensure a minimum condensing
temperature. Generally speaking, the minimum condensing temperature is necessary
in order for the chiller cycle to be able to reject heat in the condenser. It is possible
to ensure this minimum value when a partial (hot) brine ow out of the condenser
is return to the condenser inlet and mixed with the (cold) brine forward to the
condenser. By appropriately controlling the bypass, the temperature of brine into
the condenser can be kept at the necessary temperature. If the brine ow from the

dry coolers is low, the UA-value of the heat exchanger can be maintained by the
increased brine circulation through the bypass.
A pump is placed in the bypass to control the ow. If this pump has a variable fre-

(PT) connected between the brine forward and return at the condenser. In addition
a check valve is installed to ensure no unintended ow in the wrong direction.
At the free cooler the bypass is used as a frost protection system. The principle is
the same as for the condenser. The setpoint will be dierent, and the same goes for
the dimensioning of the components.
A typical brine used for this type of system is a solution with 30% propylene glycol.
The freezing point for medium is -12.8◦C .
Electric chiller
The electric chiller is designed as a conventional vapour compression cycle as de-
scribed in section 2.4. The refrigerant is ammonia, which today is a popular natural
refrigerant for larger chillers. The compressor is expected to be an open-type, which
is typical for large ammonia systems [59].
In reality the vaporisation of the refrigerant is handled in a ooded evaporator
23
==PAGE:40==
2.5. System design
(not illustrated in gure 2.10), which is common for large ammonia chillers. This
technique implies an extra component, the low pressure receiver. The principle of
the ooded evaporator is shown in gure 2.11.

Figure 2.11: Simplied schematic of a ooded evaporator.
As seen from the illustration the low pressure receiver separates the liquid refrigerant
from the gaseous. The liquid level in the receiver is controlled by an appropriate
oat valve. The vertical column in the bottom of the receiver ensures that only
liquid enters the evaporator. The circulation through the receiver and evaporator
occurs by natural means. In contrast to direct expansion systems, this technology
have no superheat after the evaporator, and the quality of the refrigerant leaving
the evaporator is a vapour/liquid mixture. The separation of gas and liquid in the
receiver ensures that only saturated vapour is drawn into the compressor suction line.
The benets from this evaporator type is no need for precise control of superheat,
as well as an enhanced average heat transfer coecient in the evaporator [23].

From gure 2.12 it should be noted that the expanded refrigerant return from the

DC distribution water
The network of dark blue streams in gure 2.10 is the piping for distribution water
to the DC system. The main principle is that the return water can ow dierently
according to the intended plant operation. This is controlled by modulating electric
valves, on/o electric valves, pumps etc. The basic functionalities of the DC water
ow conguration in the plant is described below.
If only free cooling is used, the full stream of return water from the distribution
enters "Free cooling HEX". The chilled water enters the buer tank from the top,
and nally it is drawn from the bottom of the buer tank to the DC supply line.
When the cooling demand is only supplied by the chiller (mechanical cooling) the
full return ow enters the evaporator, in which it is cooled to the desired supply
temperature. From here it is pumped to the DC supply line.
At conditions where only partial free cooling is used, the full DC return ow is
rstly sent through the free cooler, in which it is pre-cooled. After this, the stream
is pumped to the evaporator where the remaining cooling occurs.
The cooling demand is normally changing in a pace that cannot be followed by the
equipment in the cooling plant. Hence, a buer tank is installed between the return
and supply line. When cooling is only mechanical, or a combination of free and
mechanical cooling, the buer tank can be used as a decoupling of the distribution
network and the cooling plant.
If production is larger than the demand, excess
chilled water can enter the buer tank from the bottom and the level of cold water
in the buer will increase. In situations where demand is suddenly increasing, chilled
water from the buer is extracted from the bottom, meanwhile chillers will have time
to the demand for increased cooling load.
2.5.2
Cooling plant with ATES
For a cooling plant with integrated ATES technology more complexity is naturally
added to the system design. Figure 2.13 shows a PI diagram of a proposed cooling
plant with integrated ATES.
The main structure is similar to the conventional cooling plant presented in section
2.5.1, with a brine loop for connection to the dry coolers, an electric chiller facility,
a free cooler, and the DC water side connecting to the distributing network.
In addition to this two extra heat exchangers are introduced for coupling of the
groundwater ow to the system. The groundwater ow (depicted with light blue
lines) starts at the groundwater pumps located in the wells shown to the right. A
dedicated "cold" and a "hot" well forms a well-pair. Both groundwater extraction
and injection is possible in each of the two wells. In an ATES cooling situation the
groundwater is pumped from the cold well, and it can then be utilised to pre-cool

enables that the groundwater is cooled by the cold ambient air.

installed capacity can be used in a exible manner for the dierent components to
be cooled. It also illustrates that some of the capacity can be shut of at part load.
2.6
Plant operation modes
It is important to carefully organised the operation of the cooling plant, in order
to obtain the highest eciencies for the cooling production. Based on the system
designs presented in section 2.5 a description of the operation modes applied for this
thesis are given here.
The reference system is using ambient air as the only cold source. Hence, the overall
26
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 2. Methodology
approach is that the operation of the system is determined from the ambient air
temperature. An overall operation scheme is developed for the reference system.
This will also form the basis for the operation of the ATES system. Dierent ad-
ditional operation methods are suggested for the ATES system, and these will be
analysed via thermodynamic simulations and economic calculations.
2.6.1
Overall operation scheme
The main operation scheme of the reference and the ATES system is following
is divided in three.
Depending on the ambient air temperature, an appropriate
operation mode is chosen. The three modes are listed below.
1. 100% free cooling: Only the free cooler is in operation.
2. Combi cooling: The free cooler is used for pre-cooling, and the remainder of
the cooling demand is supplied by the chiller.
3. 100% mechanical cooling: Only the chiller is in operation
This operation scheme is following the principle of what is currently used in other
DC systems [58,60]. If the air is cold enough the free cooling is preferred.
Two temperature limits will dene three air temperature ranges corresponding to
the operation methods listed above. 100% free cooling occurs from the minimum
ambient air temperature up to the rst limit dened by equation 2.2.
Tfree,max = TDC,sup −∆Tfree −∆Tdry
(2.2)
Where ∆Tfree is the temperature dierence in the free cooler, and ∆Tdry is the
temperature dierence in the dry coolers.
The combi cooling mode is used when the temperature is above the rst limit and
below the second limit dened by equation 2.3.
Tcombi,max = TDC,ret −∆Tfree −∆Tdry
(2.3)
In the range of air temperature from Tcombi,max to the maximum air temperature the
operation mode is 100% mechanical cooling.
2.6.2
Suggested operation modes for ATES
The ATES technology is here considered to be an add-on to the technology used in a
conventional cooling plant, as described in 2.5.2. Dierent approaches for utilisation
of groundwater is possible with the proposed design of the DC system with integrated
ATES. Three dierent operation strategies are investigated in this thesis, and each
of these scenarios are listed below.
1. Peak load operation: Groundwater is used for pre-cooling in hours where the
demand is above a specied limit.
2. Continuous operation: Groundwater is continuously used for pre-cooling for a
specied period of time.
27
==PAGE:44==
2.7. Simplication of systems
3. Condenser heat operation: Groundwater is used as a heat sink for condenser
heat from the chiller for a specied period of time.
For the rst and second scenario the "Ground-water HEX 1" (refer to gure 2.13) is
used for pre-cooling and "Ground-water HEX 2" is used for recharge of the aquifer
in the winter season. For the third scenario only "Ground-water HEX 2" is used.
The groundwater extraction ow rates applied for each of the scenarios are assumed
on the basis of section 2.1.4. The values are presented in section 2.8.2. For the
peak load scenario dierent denitions of peak demand are investigated. Under the
continuous scenario the groundwater cooling is supplied in the summer season from
May to October (6 months). In the condenser heat scenario the groundwater is used
as heat sink from May to August (4 months).
2.7
Simplication of systems
The cooling systems described in section 2.5 are made in the attempt to imitate a
real system design and the involved technologies, as well as for illustration of the
functionalities that such a system enables. With the common brine arrangement
shown in gure 2.10 and 2.13, it is possible to utilise the same dry cooler installed
capacity for all heat exchangers that are to be cooled by the ambient air. This neces-
sitates that several pumps, modulating electric valves etc. are properly controlled.
In reality this is normally handled with a supervisory control and data acquisition
(SCADA) system.
From a thermodynamic performance perspective the specic details regarding ow
congurations for the secondary refrigerant are of smaller interest. The brine acts
as a medium for transferring heat from the groundwater exchanger/free cooler/con-
denser, to the ambient air. By assuming a well controlled brine system, this will not
be critical to the performance of the plant.
In order to avoid too high complexity with regards to controls, shifting partial
ows etc. for the thermodynamic simulations, a simplied version of the cooling
systems are set up and used in the simulation models.
The simplied systems
can be described as being assigned with some hypothetical components, in which
the eects of the dry coolers and brine loop is included. This is without actual
calculations for the brine ow. The simplication is illustrated in gure 2.14 for the
ATES system. The same principle is applied for the reference system.
As indicated in the gure, the condenser, the free cooler, and the groundwater
heat exchanger are each modelled separately as a simplied air cooled component.
The inlet temperature of the air is adjusted by adding a temperature dierence
representing the dry cooler. It is assumed that the necessary fan capacity for each
component is provided from a total clustered dry cooler capacity installed for the
system. This corresponds to how it would be in the real system with a common
brine loop that connects all heat exchanges to the total dry cooler cluster. The
energy consumption for fans is calculated on the basis of this assumption.
The energy consumption for the brine pumps hidden by the simplied components
is calculated from appropriate correlations. Details on the calculations can be found
in section 2.8.2.

2.8
Thermodynamic simulation model
The simulation model for the energy and performance analysis is made in Engineer-
ing Equation Solver (EES). The principles of the model and the assumptions used
for simulations are presented in this section.
2.8.1
Model principle and structure
The yearly perspective of this thesis is important for the creation of an appropriate
model to use for thermodynamic simulations. The model is constructed with the
ability to continuously make calculations for every hour during a year. This requires
that the model is exible, and that logic statements are used to choose the correct
operation mode automatically.
The overall operation mode, i.e. 100% free, combi, or 100% mechanical cooling, is
automatically selected in the model on the basis of the ambient temperature and
the limits dened in section 2.6.1. For simplicity, a separate script is made for the
three investigated ATES operation methods described in section 2.6.2.
29
==PAGE:46==
2.8. Thermodynamic simulation model
The general structure of the model is illustrated in gure 2.15, where a simple ow
chart shows the overall connections between main components of the script.

Figure 2.15: Flow chart illustrating the general structure of the simulation model made in EES.
The Subprogram is an EES-functionality that makes it possible to dene separate
programs within the script, and then call them only when needed. These are used
to dene separate sets of equations for the free cooler and the chiller. Three sub-
programs are written, i.e. one for each of the overall operation modes.
All input values with yearly variations are included as list of hourly values, i.e. 8760
values for a full year.
The Procedure in EES is functionality in which assignment statements are used in
stead of conventional EES-code. Hence, procedures allow the use of logic statements.
This is used here so that the plant conguration is set correctly in accordance with
the current operation mode.
Several procedures are dened for conguration of
groundwater ow, DC distribution water, temperatures etc. The main advantage
of the procedures is that it is possible to call subprograms. The logic statements in
the procedure are used to determine which of the subprograms should be called.
2.8.2
Calculations and assumptions
System performance
The performance of the systems are evaluated on the basis of the coecient of
performance (COP). For every hour in the year, the current COP is calculated as
the sum of all cooling capacity ( ˙Q) divided by the sum of all energy consumptions
( ˙W), as given in equation 2.4.
COP =
˙Qcool
˙W
(2.4)
Note that
˙Qcool and
˙W is total values for contributions included for the COP-
calculation. The contributions included can be adjusted, such that COP's for indi-
vidual technologies in the cooling plant can be calculated.
Based on the yearly perspective of the simulations, it is important to compare sys-
tems and technologies on a yearly basis. This is obtained by using the weighted
yearly average of the COP. This means that the COP is now calculated as the sum
30
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 2. Methodology
of cooling energy produced over a full year, divided by the sum of the energy used.
The yearly COP is hence calculated according to equation 2.5.
COPyear =
P8760

˙Qcool,i
P8760
i=1
˙Wi
(2.5)
Where the index i is hours. Calculations are made on an hourly basis, which means
that capacity (e.g. in [kW]) and energy (e.g. in [kWh]) can be used interchangeably.
Brine loop and dry coolers
The simplication of the brine side of the system is justied by an adjustment of
the temperature of air entering the heat exchangers. The adjustment corresponds
to a typical value of temperature dierence between ambient air and brine out of
the dry coolers. This is given by equation 2.6.
Tair,in = Tamb + ∆Tdry
(2.6)
By using this approach the main eect of the dry coolers are included in the model,
even though the brine loop is not modelled.
The electricity consumption of the pumps in the brine loop, is included by using the
relation stated in equation 2.7 [61].
˙Wp,unit
˙Qunit
= f ˙Wmin · ∆Tunit
(2.7)
Where subscript unit refers to a given heat exchanging unit, and f ˙Wmin is a constant
dened for situations where minimum power consumption at a given cooling capacity
is desired.
A pump contribution from the brine side is calculated for each heat
exchanger (condenser, free cooler, and groundwater exchanger).
The fan power at 100% dry cooler capacity is calculated with a typical percentage
of the maximum capacity, see equation 2.8. The value
˙Wfans,100 = ffans · ˙Qdry,100
(2.8)
It is assumed that the fans are variable speed, and the total fan power is thus
calculated from equation 2.9 [62].
˙Wfans = ˙Wfans,100 · CAP 2.8
fans
(2.9)
Where CAPfans = ˙Vair,tot/ ˙Vair,100. The maximum volume ow rate in the dry coolers
is calculated on the basis of the design conditions, i.m. maximum cooling load and
ambient temperature. Based on the assumptions presented in 2.7 the fan power
consumption is also calculated separately for each of the components.
Because
the brine loop connects all heat exchanger to the same cluster of dry coolers, it is
assumed that the unit fan power can be calculated as in equation 2.10.
˙Wfans,unit =

Chiller cycle
The condensation temperature needs to be controlled in o-design situation.
In
practice this can be achieved by dierent means. For the simulation model, it is
assumed that the condensation temperature follows an optimal value corresponding
to equation 2.11.
Tcond,opt = Tair,in + ∆Tcond
(2.11)
Here Tair,in is the temperature of air entering an air cooled condenser. Since the
brine loop is neglected in the simulation model, this temperature is assumed to be
elevated by the temperature dierence in the excluded dry cooler, as described in
previous section. ∆Tcond is a specied temperature dierence that denes the set
point for Tcond. The relation for the optimal condensation temperature given in
equation 2.11 is found to be generally accepted in literature [62,65,66]. The stated
value of the temperature dierence is varying from 5-15K in publications, and here
the value will be set to be ∆Tcond = 10K, which is given as a typical value for
variable speed fans [62].
The above described relation between ambient and condensing temperature will be
impractical at low ambient temperatures. If the condensing temperature is outside
the lower boundary of the operating envelope, it will not be possible to obtain the
desired condensation of refrigerant on the high pressure side of the cycle. Hence,
the chiller has a minimum operating condensation temperature, which is assumed
to be 17◦C , based on information on the commercially available ChillPac systems
by Johnson Controls [67]. This means that the applied relation for the condensing
temperature can be formulated as given in equation 2.12.
Tcond =
(
Tair,in + 10K,
for Tair,in > 7◦C
17◦C,
for Tair,in ≤7◦C
(2.12)
A ooded evaporator is installed in the chiller (please refer to gure 2.11). The
relationship in equation 2.13 applies to the cooling load in the evaporator.
˙Qevap = ˙mevap
ncirc
· (hv,sat −hl,sat) = ˙mr · (hv,sat −hin)
(2.13)
Where ˙mevap is the refrigerant mass ow through the ooded evaporator, hv,sat is
saturated gas/vapour in the receiver, hl,sat is enthalpy saturated liquid, ˙mr is the
32
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 2. Methodology
refrigerant mass ow through the compressor, and hin is the enthalpy of refrigerant
entering the receiver. ncirc is the circulation number, which is dened as the inverse
of the quality of refrigerant out of the evaporator, ncirc = 1/xevap,out.
The ooded evaporator means that there is no superheat in the evaporator. Still,
the refrigerant will experience a superheat (∆Tsh) in the compressor suction line,
due to heat transfer from the warm compressor. A pressure drop (∆Psl) in the
suction line is unavoidable.
The evaporation temperature is normally calculated as a mean of all temperature
dierences along the heat exchanger, which is given by the logarithmic mean tem-
perature dierence. Please see equation 2.14.
∆Tlm = ∆T1 −∆T2
ln(∆T1/∆T2)
(2.14)
Where ∆T1 = TDC,in −Tevap and ∆T2 = TDC,out −Tevap. In order to avoid compu-
tational issues with the logarithm in the simulation model, an alternative equation
for the mean temperature dierence is used. The used method is the Chen Mean
(CM) [68], which is generally accepted as on of the best approximations to the
logarithmic mean [69]. Equation 2.15 shows the expression for the CM.
∆TCM =

(2.15)

side is the limiting side with respect to the overall heat transfer coecient U, due
to large variations in the ow rate depending on the DC demand. With the above
equation for the mean temperature dierence, a design evaporation temperature
(Tevap,dim), design values for inlet and outlet temperature on the water side (TDC,ret
and TDC,sup), and the maximum evaporator load ( ˙Q100) it is possible to determine
the UA-value of the evaporator at 100% load from equation 2.16.
˙Qevap,100 = UA100 · ∆TCM
(2.16)

Free cooler
The free cooler is modelled by dening a minimum pinch temperature dierence in
one of the ends in the heat exchanger. The capacity stream C = ˙m · cp [kW/K]
is evaluated for the two streams (i.e. DC water and air in the simplied model),
and the pinch point temperature is dened for the outlet side of the stream wit the
lowest capacity stream.
The value of the pinch point in the free cooler is assumed to be ∆T = 2.5K [63].
Distribution side of cooling plant
The conditions of the forward and return distribution pipes are assumed to be
constant. The demand variations over the year are reected by a changing ow
rate on the distribution network. The distribution mass ow rate ( ˙mdist) is thus
determined from equation 2.19.
˙Qdem = ˙mdist·cp,DC·(TDC,ret −TDC,sup) ⇒˙mdist =
˙Qdem
cp,DC · (TDC,ret −TDC,sup) (2.19)
Where ˙Qdem is the cooling demand, cp,DC is the heat capacity of the distribution
water, TDC,ret is the return temperature, and TDC,sup is the supply temperature.
By specifying the pressures of the distribution supply an return, a total pressure
drop over the distribution network is assumed. The secondary pumps (distribution
pumps) should hence be able to overcome this pressure drop. The power consump-
tion for distribution pumps is calculated from equation 2.20.
˙Wp,dist = ˙Vdist · PDC,sup −PDC,ret
ηp
(2.20)
Where ˙Vdist is the volume ow rate of distribution water in units of m3/s, PDC,sup
and PDC,ret are pressures in [kPa], and ηp is the total eciency of the distribution
pumps.

water to the evaporator, free cooler and groundwater pre-cooler, are include in the
same manner as for the brine pumps. Please nd the relation i equation 2.7.

2.8. Thermodynamic simulation model
A dynamic head HD for the well is calculated to obtain the total head of the well
Htot. The groundwater heat exchangers are modelled similarly to the procedure
described for the free cooler.
It is necessary that the system modelling takes into account the seasonal change
in operation mode for the ATES system and the storage of heat in the aquifer.
After a cooling season, it is assumed that the groundwater in the hot well will have
a temperature corresponding to the average temperature injected in the hot well
during all cooling hours in the year. This temperature will be the basis for the
recharge operation. For the sake of simplicity, it is assumed that the groundwater is
recharged (cooled down) to the average natural occurring groundwater temperature,
which is Tgw,avg = 10.27◦C.
The general approach for the recharging of the aquifer is that the energy should be
balanced. Balances with respect to the pumped volumes have not been included in
the analysis.
Volume ow rates of extracted groundwater ( ˙Vgw) for one well-pair and temperature
limits (Tamb,rech,max) for recharge applied in the considered scenarios are given in
table 2.9. The volume ow rates are assumed to be constant, which means that
operation of the well pumps can be regarded as on/o.
The highest values are
selected for the peak load as this scenario will have fewer hours of operation, and
the two other scenarios are set to the low end of what is found to be realistic in
section 2.1. This adjustment is considered to be necessary in order to avoid the
thermal break through for the well pairs (section 1.4.5).

Air ow rates
The air ow rate through the dry coolers are normally temperature controlled. For
the simulation model a simple air ow scheme is applied, which refers to the ambient
air temperature. Since the cooling systems are modelled without the brine loop, an
air ow scheme is separately applied for each of the components that transfer heat
away from the system. It is then assumed that dry coolers in reality supply the total
required air ow at a given operation mode and ambient temperature.
The required air ows for each component is evaluated at appropriate ambient tem-
peratures, and a linear correlation is then applied in between. The correlations also
take into account the cooling demand at dierent ambient temperatures. The air
36
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 2. Methodology
ows are calculated on the basis of an assumed allowable ∆Tair over the exchanger,
as well as percentages for the share of cooling demand at the dierent air temper-
atures. The assumptions made for calculations related to determination of the air
ow schemes can be found in appendix B.1.
For the conventional cooling plant (reference system) the applied air ow rates are
illustrated in gure 2.16. The air ow to the free cooler is evaluated at min. ambient
temperature during the year, max. temperature for 100% free cooling, as well as
at max. temperature for combi cooling. The air ow to the condenser is evaluated
at min. temp for combi cooling, max. temperature for combi cooling, and max.
ambient temperature during the year.

In the 100% free cooling mode the necessary air ow is calculated to follow the
dashed blue line down to the minimum ambient air temperature. But in order to
take into account the extra energy consumption for a frost protection system that
would be installed in reality, it is assumed that the air ow to the free cooler is
constant at air temperatures below -4◦C .
This threshold is determined from a
minimum air temperature into the free cooler of 0◦C , which occurs when Tair =
TDC,freeze −∆Tdry = 0◦C−4K = −4◦C, where Tw,freeze is the freezing point of water
and ∆Tdry is the temperature dierence in the dry coolers.
It is evident that the ow rate increases signicantly when the operation mode is
100% mechanical. The interdependency between ambient air temperature and cool-
ing demand is the main reason for the steeply inclining condenser air in this operation
mode. That is, at the ambient temperatures constituting the operation modes 100%
free cooling and combi cooling, the demand is only at base load level, whereas under
100% mechanical cooling the demand will start to go up and reach maximum de-
mand (5.91MW) at max. ambient temperature of 32.1◦C . More specically gure

2.9. Economic analysis
2.7 illustrates that the temperature dependent cooling load starts at Tair = 10◦C.
This illustrates that the comfort (i.e. temperature dependent) part of the cooling
demand is signicantly aecting the energy requirements for fans in the dry coolers.
This is also why the vertical increase seen at the transition between combi and 100%
mechanical cooling is necessary.

2.9
Economic analysis
An economic evaluation is made for the DC system with integrated ATES, as well as
for the reference system. For this purpose, a discounted cash ow model is developed
and used to analyse the case from a private-economic perspective, with a simplied
socio-economic calculations for comparison. The analysis will consider the full as-
sumed lifetime of the system. This means that the conditions of system operation
will inuence the economics in the project, due to dierences in necessary installed
capacity and electricity consumption. Even though a combination of the scenarios
is possible in reality, it is here assumed for simplicity that the projects are mutually
exclusive, i.e. only one of them can be undertaken for the specic case area. Hence,
three sets of calculations are made for the DC system with integrated ATES, cor-
responding to each of the investigated operation modes. The three ATES scenarios
are compared to identify the most economically attractive operation scheme, and
furthermore compared to the reference system.
38
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 2. Methodology
2.9.1
Economic key indicators
The economic analysis serves to nd out if it is economically feasible to invest in
the integration of ATES and DC for the considered case area. By using a set of
common economic indicators, it is possible to identify if each of the scenarios are
economically feasible, and at the same time assess which scenario is most attractive.
The economic indicators used for this analysis are payback time, net present value,
the protability index, and the internal rate of return.
Theory related to these
indicators are presented below [75].
Payback time
The payback time (PBT) is a fairly simplied indicator that considers cumulative
cash ows. The PBT provides the number of years after which the investment is
repaid from the expected future cash ows related to the project. For each year dur-
ing the expected system lifetime (LT) the cumulative cash ow (CCF) is calculated
as given in equation 2.22.
CCFi = CCFi−1 + CFi
(2.22)
Where subscript i = [0; LT] is an index for the time period number, and CF refers
to the cash ow of a given year. The year number of the last year with a negative
cumulative cash ow (yearneg) and the corresponding value (CCFneg), as well as
the value of the rst positive value (CCFpos), are now identied. The PBT is then
calculated from equation 2.22.
PBT = yearneg + 1 −
CCFpos
CCFpos −CCFneg
(2.23)
Net present value
The idea of the "present value"-term is to adjust all future cash ows in an invest-
ment project, so that the values reect today's worth. This adjustment is handled
by using the discount factor shown in equation 2.24.
DTt =
1
(1 + r)t
(2.24)
Where r is the discount rate, and t is the time period number. A discount factor is
calculated for each time period in the investment analysis. These are then multiplied
with the ordinary cash ow of the respective years to obtain the discounted cash
ows. The net present value (NPV) is then calculated by summing discounted cash
ows for all future years, and adding the (negative) initial investment. This is stated
in equation 2.25.
NPV = −CF0 +
LT
X
t=1
CFt · DFt
(2.25)
Where CF0 is the initial investment, LT is the lifetime, and CFt is the cash ow for
year t.
39
==PAGE:56==
2.9. Economic analysis
Internal rate of return
The internal rate of return (IRR) describes the expected yearly return from invest-
ment. By denition, if the IRR is equal to the discount rate, the NPV is zero. An
IRR larger than the discount rate means that the NPV is larger than zero, and the
economical feasibility can hence be assessed by comparing the IRR to the discount
rate.
Protability index
The IRR does not take the scale of the investment into account, which can be an
issue when comparing dierent system alternatives like in this thesis. An indicator
which is independent of scales of investments is the protability index (PI). The PI
adjust for the dierent investment sizes, by simply dividing the NPV of operating
cash ows (NPVOCF) with the initial investment (CF0), as given in equation 2.26.
PI = NPVOCF
CF0
(2.26)
The NPVOCF is calculated on the basis of all cash ows except for year zero where
the initial investment occurs.
This indicator is independent of the scale of the
investment, which means that it can be rightfully used for comparison of project
alternatives with dierent initial investments. The higher the PI, the more attractive
it is. If its value is above one the project is economically feasible.
2.9.2
System prices and assumptions
Investment costs and operation and maintenance (O&M) costs are dicult to pre-
dict. It is out of the scope of this thesis to obtain specic oers from contractors and
similar, and hence the prices will be based on typical values. The tool "Fjernkøl 2.0"
is developed for economical assessment of DC and ATES projects, among others.
The tool provides some typical values for costs related to investments and O&M
for DC and ATES projects. In general, these suggested values will be used for the
economic analysis.
General economic assumptions
Firstly it should be stated that several general economic parameters will inuence
the future cash ows related to the project. Future trends for these parameters are
unknown, and thus the used values for this economic analysis are based on best
guesses.
Table 2.11 provides an overview of the assumed values for general economic param-
eters.
The ination rate is assumed to be constant over the lifetime of the plant. The
used discount rate for socio-economic perspective corresponds to current values for
Denmark used by Danish Energy Agency [76], and this value is determined for cash
ows without ination (real). The private-economic discount rate includes the extra
risks for the private investor as well as ination, and is therefore higher than for
the socio-economics. The value is normally determined internally in a company to

Price of electricity and taris
The electricity price is another unknown to which some assumptions must be dened.
